1416596585|5.0|Google has both transformed the Internet and become a verb synonymous with 'search.' \\"In the Plex\\" is the product of author Steven Levy's having spent five years attending internal Google meetings and meeting with hundreds of employees. The book's content covers Google from its origination up through its latest management reshuffling attempt to reduce bureaucracy and speed decision-making. Readers will be impressed by the strategic muddling (eg. Google's beginning without any sense of a business plan; Overture's failure to patent its AdWord predecessor - allowing Google free access to the concepts), mistakes (eg. initially trying to sell the company for $1.6 million to Yahoo, DEC/AltaVista, and Excite so the founders could return to Stanford; going against China's government; attempting to build an on-line searchable database of books - rejected on legal grounds; keeping the details of its data center secret), and masterful moves (eg. the thinking underlying Android,  Adwords, and PageRank; the conduct of its IPO), its emphasis on recruiting extremely qualified staff (often PhDs in new computer-science fields) and ability to use them well (eg. allowing 20% of work time to be spent on self-selected projects), the firm's ability to both significantly lower costs while increasing speed, and its emphasis on data-driven decision-making - despite projects often initiated by a whim from either of its brainiac founders.<br /><br />The term 'Google' came from a misspelling of the term 'googol,' meaning 1 followed by 100 zeroes. Early 1998 search queries sometimes took ten seconds, while dealing with only 50 million sites. Results returned were often obviously erroneous (eg. confusing 'bio'  in a context denoting 'biological' instead of 'biography'), combinations of words (eg. 'New York' vs. 'New York' + 'Times,' or + 'New York' + 'Times Square,' and 'rock' vs. 'rock' + 'little'), and spam. Today's searches return much more accurate results in hundredths of a second and reference billions of sites. Improvements came from automatically including references to synonyms, incorporating the user's geographical location, changing the programming language and algorithms, hardware revisions (eg. increased use of solid-state instead of disc memory), and increased hardware.<br /><br />Google now has an estimated 24 data centers around the world - each holding at least $1 billion worth of low-cost 'home-made' PCs managed mostly by redundancy-providing software (instead of the typical more expensive and reliable servers), located in areas to minimize power costs (eg. near the Columbia River/Bonneville Power, colder climates that reduce/eliminate the need for A/C), using its redundancy capabilities to run the centers hotter (less energy costs), and using Google-owned fiberglass 'pipes' bought at a steep discount after the dot-com crash. Overall, Levy contends that Google data center costs are only one-third that of others when built.<br /><br />The importance of speed to success was confirmed when Google tripled the speed of its web-based photo services, and saw a 40% usage increase the day of implementation - despite no public notification of the improvement. The value of free services that help Google advertisers was demonstrated when Google bought Urchin in 2005, revised it into 'Google Analytics' so marketers could track site visitors from all sources (including links within PDF documents) to determine which ads were working best, according to eg. frequency viewed, sales, time spent on site. Google AdWords revenue increased about $3 billion after Google Analytics' implementation because buyers felt more confident about their expenditures. Similarly, it has gone on to provide 'Conversion Optimizer,' another free tool that provides automatic bid management for AdWords customers by analyzing data from both the target and similar advertisers, geographic origin information, etc.<br /><br />Google search results are frequently improved through studying instances where users quickly decided to return to the search page, and when the item they selected to examine was far from the #1 result returned. Over 600 algorithm changes were implemented in 2009. Changes are often tested in an 'A/B' manner in which about 1% of users access the changed algorithm, and their reactions compared to remainder using the old algorithm. Google has also compared search engine use, with and without ads, and found that users prefer results that include relevant ads - separate from the search results.<br /><br />Early on Google's founders saw language translation as a way to expand knowledge, and then hired a German with a recent PhD in the area of computer translation. Following IBM's lead, he fed large amounts of identical text in different languages into algorithms designed to learn language nuances and how words/sentences correspond from one language to another to ultimately produce translations. It now offers translation between over 50 languages - eg. allowing businesses to advertise across countries, researchers to draw from a much larger base of information, and Google Android phone users to better communicate in foreign lands. (Android was also originally an acquired entity.)<br /><br />Google has also shown it is not afraid to borrow from others - either via buying other companies (eg. Urchin), adopting from others (eg. AdWords grew out of competitor Overture's approach), or learning from others' mistakes (eg. users disliked GoTo's mixing paid ads within organic search results - Google's AdWords instead put them on the right side of the page, or at the very top).<br /><br />Providing free 411 directory assistance service to phone users allowed Google to build an effective voice analyzer that now serves up text messages from voicemail; en route it also collected revenue for providing relevant ads to users.<br /><br />Internet users frequently become concerned over privacy issues - Facebook being the most infamous in this regard. Google also has had problems - concerns over its street view (pictures from the street), facial recognition application, Google Earth (acquired - originally Earth Viewer), retention of individual user search information, and inserting relevant ads into gmail. Resolution of these concerns is an on-going activity/problem.<br /><br />Early after its founding, venture capitalist John Doerr convinced founders Page and Brin to hire a business-savvy CEO by exposing them to Steve Jobs, Andy Grove, Jeff Bezos, etc. While Jobs was their first choice (unavailable), Doerr convinced them to instead hire Eric Schmidt. Schmidt had a PhD in computer science from Berkeley, was co-author of a well-liked coding tool (lex), had been Chief Technology Officer at Sun Microsystems, and CEO of Novell. This month Schmidt become Google's executive chairman (focus on partnerships, customers, government outreach), and was replaced as CEO by co-founder Larry Page. Pundits contend that the unsaid reason for the change is that Apple's recent revenue and earnings growth has been about 2.5 times faster than Google's (eg. Google passed on the opportunity to develop Twitter - it now reportedly is in competition with Facebook to buy it). Facebook is also seen as pressuring Google - especially in improved data center power efficiency (using higher voltage to reduce transformer energy losses from about 23% to 7%, and server designs that allow use of cheaper 'swamp coolers' instead of A/C - despite the higher humidity. Facebook also has released its new data center design details, allowing anyone else to use them, creating increased scale economies for all - thereby reducing Google's current cost advantage and leaving it with huge sunk costs. Finally, Levy adds that Facebook has accumulated information on hundreds of millions of users - their likes, and specific characteristics. As of now, Google cannot access such information. (Google's current '+1 initiative,' analogous to Facebook's 'Like,' is an attempt to counter this important missed opportunity - registered +1 users are given the opportunity to easily recommend specific Web links that come up in search, even websites accessed without Google's help.)<br /><br />Every new Google hire must be approved by co-founder Larry Page. The entire process averages about 60 days. Technical applicants' references and academic records are checked, and up to 8 interviews with tough technical or engineering questions are required. Prior to final approval the applicant's file is also reviewed by a hiring council, deciding whether to forward the applicant to Page, or not.<br /><br />In the early days of the iPhone (2008), Google noticed that owners of these phones were doing a huge number of Web searches - despite Web pages being slow to load on phones. Google began working on ways to search by talking or taking photos (eg. take photos of movie posters to pull up trailers; can also eg. translate photos of foreign menus), and now has a 97% market share for mobile searches.<br /><br />Example of a simple, but important strategic move: Google Toolbar places a Google search box atop a user's browser as a corporate defense against Microsoft putting its own search engine (Bing) into its Explorer browser. The incentive for users to do so - the browser also provides a pop-up blocker. Another example, conceptually simple, but no operationally, is Google's envisioning that the future of search will be on mobile devices - hence Google's foray into smartphones via Android. (Mobile ads, however, sell for less than half the price of Web ads.)<br /><br />Google's Android strategy has been somewhat self-defeating and is somewhat of an enigma. Android is an operating system designed for cell phones, developed by a firm that Google bought 7/2005. Some envision Google's purpose for Android, given away free in typical Google fashion (typically the carrier pays about 20% of a phone's cost for the operating system), is to undermine Apple's dominance of the smart-phone application market through also providing users with valuable free applications (eg. free directions to drivers, free international calls and texting, quickly locating requested types of businesses, etc., gmail) Supposedly this would provide a competitive advantage and turn the phone revenue market towards advertising, and Apple's disadvantage. (The jury's still out.) Early on Google also tried directly selling unlocked phones using Android, hoping carriers would give those buyers a discount on service. (They didn't, and probably resent the direct and potential losses in revenue.)<br /><br />At this time (5/1/11) there are about 170 devices that run Android OS, vs. 5 for Apple's OS. Android gives carriers 30% of app sales (less credit car fees), vs. Apple keeping this amount (developer gets 70%). Apple vets applications before they are allowed, and Google does not; has sold about $3 billion worth, vs. $100 million for Android.<br /><br />Author Levy contends that Google's social media efforts have been a comedy of errors. It lost the bid for Facebook's advertising contract to Microsoft, and instead had to settle for contracting with MySpace. It blew an opportunity to start a Friendster-type site in 2002, called Orkut.com that quickly dominated Brazil and India - by the time Google revamped operations to speed U.S. performance to acceptable performance, Facebook was on the rise. It also passed up buying Twitter early in that company's history.<br /><br />Finally, no examination of Google would be complete without reflecting on its sometimes seemingly haphazard strategic moves. These include buying Picasa (stores user photos free online), Google Earth (free 3D closeups), and efforts to make energy more cheaply available to consumers and itself. Activities in the latter area include $250 million-plus investments in home appliance management in collaboration with G.E., a 'field of mirrors solar plant, wind farms, government lobbying, etc. It is difficult to see how this contributes to its main line of business.<br /><br />Bottom-Line: Google is clearly an incredible company, despite missteps. As a result, its market value is close to that of Microsoft. \\"In the Plex\\" allows readers to understand why Google is so impressive, why it is fundamentally an advertising company (almost 98% of 2009 revenues came from advertising - 70% from its own websites), why its business model relies on giving away services (search, Gmail, maps, etc.) - it builds volume, and why it now is threatened by Facebook. Levy's book, however, requires additional information from other authors, Wikipedia, Google on-line sources, and current news articles to obtain full value. Regardless, it is interesting and well worth reading - if only for the strategic insights of all the players involved.+1 users are given the opportunity to easily recommend specific Web links that come up in search, even websites accessed without Google's help.)     Every new Google hire must be approved by co-founder Larry Page. The entire process averages about 60 days. Technical applicants' references and academic records are checked, and up to 8 interviews with tough technical or engineering questions are required. Prior to final approval the applicant's file is also reviewed by a hiring council, deciding whether to forward the applicant to Page, or not.       In the early days of the iPhone (2008), Google noticed that owners of these phones were doing a huge number of Web searches - despite Web pages being slow to load on phones. Google began working on ways to search by talking or taking photos (eg. take photos of movie posters to pull up trailers; can also eg. translate photos of foreign menus), and now has a 97% market share for mobile searches.     Example of a simple, but important strategic move: Google Toolbar places a Google search box atop a user's browser as a corporate defense against Microsoft putting its own search engine (Bing) into its Explorer browser. The incentive for users to do so - the browser also provides a pop-up blocker. Another example, conceptually simple, but no operationally, is Google's envisioning that the future of search will be on mobile devices - hence Google's foray into smartphones via Android. (Mobile ads, however, sell for less than half the price of Web ads.)    Google's Android strategy has been somewhat self-defeating and is somewhat of an enigma. Android is an operating system designed for cell phones, developed by a firm that Google bought 7/2005. Some envision Google's purpose for Android, given away free in typical Google fashion (typically the carrier pays about 20% of a phone's cost for the operating system), is to undermine Apple's dominance of the smart-phone application market through also providing users with valuable free applications (eg. free directions to drivers, free international calls and texting, quickly locating requested types of businesses, etc., gmail) Supposedly this would provide a competitive advantage and turn the phone revenue market towards advertising, and Apple's disadvantage. (The jury's still out.) Early on Google also tried directly selling unlocked phones using Android, hoping carriers would give those buyers a discount on service. (They didn't, and probably resent the direct and potential losses in revenue.)    At this time (5/1/11) there are about 170 devices that run Android OS, vs. 5 for Apple's OS. Android gives carriers 30% of app sales (less credit car fees), vs. Apple keeping this amount (developer gets 70%). Apple vets applications before they are allowed, and Google does not; has sold about $3 billion worth, vs. $100 million for Android.    Author Levy contends that Google's social media efforts have been a comedy of errors. It lost the bid for Facebook's advertising contract to Microsoft, and instead had to settle for contracting with MySpace. It blew an opportunity to start a Friendster-type site in 2002, called Orkut.com that quickly dominated Brazil and India - by the time Google revamped operations to speed U.S. performance to acceptable performance, Facebook was on the rise. It also passed up buying Twitter early in that company's history.    Finally, no examination of Google would be complete without reflecting on its sometimes seemingly haphazard strategic moves. These include buying Picasa (stores user photos free online), Google Earth (free 3D closeups), and efforts to make energy more cheaply available to consumers and itself. Activities in the latter area include $250 million-plus investments in home appliance management in collaboration with G.E., a 'field of mirrors solar plant, wind farms, government lobbying, etc. It is difficult to see how this contributes to its main line of business.    Bottom-Line: Google is clearly an incredible company, despite missteps. As a result, its market value is close to that of Microsoft. \\"In the Plex\\" allows readers to understand why Google is so impressive, why it is fundamentally an advertising company (almost 98% of 2009 revenues came from advertising - 70% from its own websites), why its business model relies on giving away services (search, Gmail, maps, etc.) - it builds volume, and why it now is threatened by Facebook. Levy's book, however, requires additional information from other authors, Wikipedia, Google on-line sources, and current news articles to obtain full value. Regardless, it is interesting and well worth reading - if only for the strategic insights of all the players involved.

1401309666|5.0|The Long Tail was a very interesting read and as a media and music entrepreneur in a niche market, Afro-Soul Music in French and English, his theories made a lot of sense and I was able to directly relate to them since they are applicable to my daily business reality. In the summer of 2009, I co-founded 413 Management, which is a hybrid record label and management agency. Me and my business partners had absolutely no experience as record executives and did not have access to a significant amount of capital, but what we did have was a supremely talented artist, Rafiya, and a vision to fill the void for transcultural music in this increasingly multicultural world. Rafiya is of Congolese origin (Democratic Republic of Congo), was born in Los Angeles, and was raised across West Africa and the Caribbean. She is fluent in French, English and Spanish, and incorporates these languages and more in her music. Apart from the lyrical content, her sound is a mixture of blues, jazz and soul with African rhythms. This belief in her ability to build and grow a fan-base was indirectly based on the long tail theory, as we were aware that her music caters to a particular niche. The following chapters, \\"The New Producers\\", \\"The New Markets\\" and \\"The New Tastemakers\\" are the most important ones from the book as they are the heart of the long tail's genesis. The chapter on the New Producers struck a chord as it discussed the democratization of production tools and how it led to a shift from \\"passive consumers to active producers\\". This rise in the amount of producers, led to an increase in content best suited for niches and to a lengthening of the tail. These tools empowered consumers, and new markets such as iTunes, provided them with business avenues. Because the production became democratized, the age of recommendation came to be; an age where peer reviews are sought after and have as high or higher value than reviews from the establishment and mass media. These three chapters illustrate how 413 Management is able to navigate the industry. We are new producers with talent and access to tools, we leverage new markets such as iTunes and Amazon where Rafiya's \\"Amazing\\" debut album is available for retail, and our marketing efforts are focused on encouraging people to sample, share and recommend. Her fan-base is growing organically as a result of word-of-mouth and her continued success and growth in the past two years illustrate the long tail theory in practice. I wholeheartedly recommend The Long Tail because it is easy and fun to read and provides valuable and practical business insights.

0061438294|3.0|I had high expectations for this book because I read many of the overwhelmingly positive reviews before purchasing. Yes, this book is a fairly good read, but is not a great resource for reading body language. There are a lot of 5 star reviews posted from law enforcement mentioning how essential this book is to reading body language. Honestly, I am 20 years old and have no experience in law enforcement and I still found this book to be full of basic information. I think the most important message this book sends out is to pay attention when people are talking- not only to what they're telling you, but also what their body is telling you. If you pay attention, you'll notice when someone is comfortable or uncomfortable in response to certain questions or stimuli.<br /><br />Since I found that a lot of the points made in the book are easy to pick up on and understand, I will point out some of the points I highlighted as interesting. If you have a decent amount of life experience, I think you'll find that you know as much as I do or more on the topic already.<br />- \\"When there is stress, the lips will begin to tighten and disappear\\" (Lip Compression)<br />- \\"We purse out lips or pucker when we are in disagreement with something or someone, or we are thinking of a possible alternative\\"<br />- Concealment of hands (under tables, behind objects) should be avoided because this can be perceived as uncomfortable, withdrawn, sneaky, or deceptive.<br />- Finger pointing is an offensive gesture.<br />- Steepling of hands is a sign of confidence.<br />- Thumbs sticking up, or sticking out of pockets is a display of confidence.<br />- Stroking of fingers or palms is a sign of nervousness/ low confidence.<br />- The feet are the most \\"honest\\" part of the body. How they react is most genuine, however, is often overlooked.<br /><br />There are more interesting facts mentioned in the book that make it an interesting read, but I mentioned these because they are the ones I think are most interesting (that I didn't know of previously). Navarro also adds stories of his personal experiences in numbered boxes within the text to emphasize how these techniques have helped him in the FBI and throughout his personal life. The issue I have with these numbered boxes as well as the pictures within the text is that they are referenced throughout the text, but they are not organized well in the book. I feel like the pictures should have been added directly below the paragraph mentioning a certain behavior, rather than on the next page or 2 pages over. I bought this thinking it would have more information on micro-expressions, because of my fascination with the show \\"Lie to Me\\" based on Ekman's studies, but it didn't incorporate a lot on the topic.<br /><br />Overall, it is an interesting book, but not quite as informative as I would have liked.

0316017922|5.0|Outliers is an insightful, elightnening and deeply motivating book. In a nutshell, the book's theme is this: Conventional wisdom has it that success is the outcome of a person's individual merit; the product of their innate talent, dedication and hard work. Gladwelll argues that this theory is too simplistic. He provides evidence that the oppertunities a person meets over the course of his life, the upbrining he is subjected to, the amount of effort he invests and the cultural traditions passed down to him are the key determining factors to his success.    The book is organized in two parts. The first part, titled Oppertunity, argues for the idea that successful people couldn't have achieved much if it weren't for the oppertunities that came their way. Several cases are put forward to support this hypothesis. I will take the liberty of citing two instances to clarify the point: (1) Bill Gates was among a handful few who had access to computers in the late sixties worldwide. This allowed him the very special chance of mastering computer programming while still a teen-ager. An opportunity harldy offered to anyone else. (2) Many of the giants of silicon valley, the likes of Bill Gates, Steve Jobs and Erich Schmidt, were born on just the right time (1954-1955) to allow them to make the most of the personal computer boom. Had they been a little older or a little younger they would have missed it.     The second part, titled legacy, argues for the idea that the values and traditions of a certain culture make its people either more or less qualified to succeed at certain tasks. For instance persistence and hard-work are hallmarks of the chinese culture assimilated by countless decades of tedious rice cultivation. It is instructive to note that cultivating rice requires ten to twenty times the effort needed to cultivate the same area with wheat. Owing to their culture, chinese students are more persistent and hard-working hence better than western children at such subjectsas mathematics eventhough their IQ is equivalent or, more often than not, slightly less than their western counterparts.    On the down side, two issues are in order: (1) Many would agree that the author makes very bold claims on the basis of relatively little evidence. Indeed most of the book's assertions do make sense but they are soley based on no more than a handful of cases. (2) When I reached the end of the book I couldn't help feeling \\"Hey, is that it?!\\". The amount of hype around the book raises one's expectations to such a high point that I believe most readers will end up slightly disappointed.    All said, this is one of the very best nonfiction books out there.
0817439390|5.0|I love this book.  Bryan Peterson's book is easy to read.  The book encourages you to use your camera at the end of each section.
B004EPYZUI|5.0|I initially had little interest in \\"Sucker Punch.\\"  I had seen the preview but it was not compelling.  Yet the negative reviews were so many and so hateful, and the positive reviews were so few but equally passionate, I became curious, if worried.  I actually sat where I could make a quick getaway as soon as I could no longer take it.  This was a movie I was prepared to dislike.  Instead, I fell in love with it.      It all began with Ken Kelsey and his unhappiness over how the movie \\"One Flew Over The Cuckoo's Nest\\" turned out.  I greatly admired Kesey's the book and was excited about the prospect of a movie.  Unfortunately, the producers took the easy way out.  Instead of doing it from the POV of Chief Broom and his mental issues (paranoid schizophrenia, likely), including the hallucinatory and occasional unpleasant imagery, the decision was made to tell the story objectively, using Randall Patrick Mcmurphy as the central figure.  Telling it from the POV of Chief Broom must of smacked of trip movies and the like and nobody wanted to do that.  It sounded far too risky a box-office proposition.      I was left hoping that there were people here and there in Hollywood who wanted to do the story like the author intended.  That is, tell the story of a tragic rescuer from the POV of Chief Broom himself.        I have no idea whether any of these thoughts of achieving the vision of Kesey's novel ever coursed through the mind of writer/director Zack Snyder when he was writing \\"Sucker Punch.\\"  From what I have read he mostly wanted to assemble something along the lines of a framing device that would enhance and give ironic detachment, or some such thing, to this  tale (tragic in its own right) of female empowerment.  The Battle Angels/Pretty Warriors so standard in Anime would have been a tempting symbolic choice.  Much of the story requires fantasy, this being a tale set in a mental institution.  The main character \\"Baby Doll\\" would of course use the wildest fantasies shecould summon to sustain her.      It's all in the mind, after all, the \\"threater\\" of consciousness (or of war or of movies -- \\"theater\\" works in all three contexts).  Nevertheless, Snyder's choice of setting and show wouldn't work for most (surely test audiences would have reported their dissatisfaction).  In fact, as near as I can determine, about 1/6 of the potential audience hated even the idea of the movie.  But whatever his reasoning, once the decision was made he did not hold back.  [Not that he is the kind of guy to hold back in any event, as viewers of \\"300\\" and \\"The Watchmen\\" well know.]  It was a daring move.  The relentless imagery of male monsters dominating a prison/nightclub/brothel of helpless females could easily have succumbed to parody.  What fascinated me, as I watched the film, is how able Snyder was to keep it all under control and only tearing loose during the fantasy sequences.      But do the fantasies work?  They are the visual core of the movie and Baby Doll is not a paranoid schizophrenic.  Her fantasies would be nothing like those of Chief Broom.  The issue is not if the fantasy sequences should have been cut or  reduced in scope for enhance audience acceptance.  The issue is: do they work given the character of Baby Doll and her situation?    They do.  They are plausible.  Anyone caught unjustly in such a situation (young male or young female) would have violent fantasies of fighting back.  If not against the immediate obviously enemy, impossible in this instance, than any enemy that suits the psychological purposes of the terrified.  The more oppressive the external situation, the more extreme the internal response would be.  Having gone to the lengths to portray the girl's environment as horrible as he did, he must have felt equally compelled to show how under the leadership of Baby Doll that the girls would fight back against imaginary monsters every bit as intensely as real ones.  And from that ongoing fight draw strength to keep fighting.    Though female empowerment, violent or otherwise, is a fairly common theme in hollywood, Snyder cranks up the volume to a pitch that is almost unbearable (I mean that literally -- as an experiment I saw the film in one of the \\"I-Max\\" theaters and almost had to keep my fingers in my ears throughout).  But he succeeds thereby in keeping the girl-power theme and characters from becoming cliche.    The chosen psychological symbolism won me over completely, I went with it without regret.  In addition to the Anime stylization, beautifully done, the figure of the \\"Wise Man\\" is crucial.  He is the Jungian animus for Baby Doll (who is the Jungian anima for another of the captive girls, Sweet Pea).  The \\"Wise Man\\" (wonderfully played by Scott Glenn) centers the fantasy sequences.  His appearance at the end of the film also enables the perfect emotional release.      \\"Sucker Punch\\" is a masterpiece and as such a demanding one.  While I understand most people who go to the movies expect to see \\"really wide-screen TV.\\" This requires more attention.  So I   reserve by impatience for those critics who seemed unable to watch the film without being in a rush get back to the office to see that they say the same thing as their fellow critics (I lost track of how many times \\"teen-age boys and masturbation\\" were used as the ultimate put-down.  How droll.)  If that's your profession, it is not good enough.  Critics have a responsibility to go beyond what Ebert calls \\"level-1\\" criticisms when it is clear the writer-director is putting all that he has into it.      A lot more could be written about \\"Sucker Punch\\" and will be written.  For now, to the entire cast, pitch perfect, and this wonderful, sad tale that fully realizes at last Kesey's vision of freedom, psychological and existential -- magnificently done.
B00001X591|5.0|This is a really good double CD of some of Albinoni's best music, very well performed by the invariably excellent Academy of Ancient Music under the Christopher Hogwood.<br /><br />This is rather underrated music, I think. It's certainly much more than the pleasant baroque wallpaper which many people think Albinoni to be. The adagio of Op.9 No.2, for example, is one of the loveliest movements I know in a baroque concerto. These are very fine performances, too, which bring out the verve, joy and sheer beauty of these concertos. Andrew Manze is always brilliant, but the oboists are also excellent. Frank de Bruine in particular has a lovely, flowing, effortless sound and I find that I prefer this set to my old CM90/Standage set with Anthony Robson, who by comparison seems a little plodding in his articulation and phrasing.<br /><br />It isn't surprising that Andrew Manze and Christopher Hogwood, two real giants of Baroque performance, should bring something special to these concerti and the playing of Frank de Bruine is a real bonus. It's a lovely set and very warmly recommended

I'll be at a book signing-accompanied by a nurse and by an attendant holding an ear trumpet, hooked to an IV drip feeding me liquefied nachos, wearing a lavishly embroidered jumpsuit more dazzling than anything Elvis ever wore in his Las Vegas period-and as readers greet me and receive their inscribed copies of my latest effort, a significant percentage of them will ask me to write a sequel to Watchers. 'It embodies all of the major themes with which [Koontz] has been obsessed: the healing power of love and friendship; the struggle to overcome the past and change what we are; the moral superiority of the individual over the workings of the state and large institutions; the wonder of both the natural world and the potential of the human mind; the relationship of mankind to God; transcendence; and how we sustain hope in the face of our awareness that all things die.' Those are, indeed, the fundamental issues in this novel.<br />..<br />\\"We have within us the ability to change for the better and to find dignity as individuals rather than as drones in one mass movement or another. We have the ability to love, the need to be loved, and the willingness to put our own lives on the line to protect those we love, and it is in these aspects of ourselves that we can glimpse the face of God; and through the exercise of these qualities, we come closest to a Godlike state.<br /><br />\\"With that said, my greatest hope is not that you find the themes of this novel worth analyzing, but that you find this to be a rip-roaring, rattling-good story. I hope it keeps you so far out on the edge of your chair that you have butt bruises from repeatedly falling to the floor. I hope it makes you laugh and cry. A novel can have multiple, intricately woven levels of theme and symbol, but it fails if it is not first a wonderful tale.\\"<br /><br />If you are someone who needs to know a full plot summary before you're willing to spend $10, there are plenty of reviews here that give away quite a bit. It ought to be a disaster. For reasons I can't fathom, it isn't. It is absurd, gratuitous, vulgar, childish, stupid and utterly incongruous. And I thought it was great.<br /><br />Natalie Portman playing against Danny McBride: what kind of fevered imagination comes up with that sort of an idea? What sort of miracle worker makes it actually happen? What sort of genius makes it work?<br /><br />And that's just the start: There are jarring conflicts everywhere you look. Animal husbandry: You may cross a horse with a donkey and get a mule, but some hybrids just don't go: You don't play a [[ASIN:B0041KKYEM Black Swan]] against [[ASIN:B001IBIDLI Kenny Powers]]. You don't cross [[ASIN:B000TJBNHG The Princess Bride]] with [[ASIN:B0000A02TZ Animal House]]. If you do, you sure as hell don't throw in [[ASIN:B00004Z1GI Bad Taste]] for good measure. You don't blow millions on Industrial Light & Magic style digital effects one moment and then fill the screen with a cheap rubber alien wizard (a dope-smoking, kiddie-fiddling alien wizard at that) that looks like it came from a joke shop the next. You can't rely on non-expectant swearing and expect to get away with it. At least, not fifty times. But all in all, it seems to work.<br /><br />Danny McBride - last seen heading [[ASIN:B001IBIDLI Eastbound and Down]], more or less reprises Kenny Powers, complete with mullet and goatee (no method actor, Mr McBride, I dare say), only here he is a useless younger prince in a mediaeval kingdom in a land far, far, far away. On a different planet, indeed: there is a twin moon, and it is important to the plot (I suppose you could call it a plot).<br /><br />Unlike most mediaeval kingdoms this is one where everyone intones smutty innuendos with English accents that come and go like rotating speakers. Kenny - I mean, Thadeous - is more or less [[ASIN:B00005A1ST The Black Adder]] to Fabious' Prince Harry. Fabious (James Franco, swashing his buckles for all he is worth and for some reason channelling Steve Tyler from Aerosmith) has a whale of a time as a slightly dopey hunk. They are pitted against evil warlock Leezar (a superb Justin Theroux) a criminally under-explained villain who has bad teeth, worse hair, and who also has a whale of a time, vacillating between occult power and sexual impotence (there are knob jokes aplenty here). Theroux' delivery of his actually pretty ordinary material is spot on.<br /><br />Along the way there are entirely gratuitous scenes of every dimension: sand-encrusted nude barbarian vixens in the thrall of a large fat pink sorcerer (John Fricker) in a nappy, in a Thunderdome. Much violence, and more gay innuendo. Did I mention there are lots of willie jokes? There are the occasional outrageous visual gags. There's a frisky minotaur. There is a scheduled coming together of the moons which will result in a rare event called ... - well, you'll just have to see it, because I don't want to spoil it. Totally immature, but still I just about wet myself.<br /><br />Natalie Portman boxes on far more gamely than anyone has any right to expect a recently minted Oscar laureate to. I constantly found myself wondering what on earth had possessed her to sign up for this project - perhaps relaxation therapy after a long dose of Darren Aronofsky - but being eternally grateful that she had. She is a tremendous sport throughout, and my regard for her is undoubtedly enhanced.<br /><br />I have complained in these pages about the reliance on rote, formula and technology in place of proper drama in modern motion pictures. No complaints on that score here: while there's absolutely no proper drama on speak of, you couldn't fault David Gordon Green and his team for showing a bit of spirit. Instead of a tightly followed plan, there's no plan at all (Do you think my methods are unsound? I don't see any method at all, sir).<br /><br />But when the alternative that passes for comedy these days is [[ASIN:B00177Y9ZW Meet The Spartans]] and [[ASIN:B000EXDS4S Red Riding Hood]] (At least, I can only assume that's intended to be a comedy) then this has to come well recommended.<br /><br />Leave your maiden aunt, your kids and your critical faculties at home, come for a laugh and you'll have a blast.<br /><br />Olly BuxtonBut when the alternative that passes for comedy these days is [[ASIN:B00177Y9ZW Meet The Spartans]] and [[ASIN:B000EXDS4S Red Riding Hood]] (At least, I can only assume that's intended to be a comedy) then this has to come well recommended. The creepy androgyny of the vampire in the Swedish version has been lost--she really seems like a different species of creature in the original. The bullies are also much more menacing in the first movie. At the end of that film, you really believe that they're these weird hick psychos willing to kill the little boy; here they seem more annoying than frightening. They give the main character a wedgie at the beginning of the US film; the boy screams and whines, and actually wets himself. I thought he overreacted: it was only a wedgie, after all. In the original, one gets the sense that they boy's life is unbelievably bleak, and made even more miserable by the sadistic attentions of these three bullies. It makes sense, therefore, that he would bond with the vampire girl from next door. The ugly 80s socialist environment of the original heightens one's sense of the boy's predicament. Here, the 80s environment seems like a mere schtick (fun 80s soundtrack, anyone? Yawn.). Finally, I'd heard that this movie made some key changes to the original film. Not so--many of the key scenes are almost identical. The original is ultimately a beautiful, bleak, deeply weird love story; this one's a slightly boring rehash.

1439102724|1.0|As a long-time high school English teacher, literature lover, and Jodi Picoult fan, I felt that I  must say something about this Picoult disappointment.  I've read almost all of Picoult's books and have loved most of them.  They provide great springboards for discussion among my friends and family who are also Picoult fans.  We've spent many hours discussing the merits of Picoult's writing style and subject matter and have delved into topics of great controversy, thinking about things in ways we've never thought before.  We've talked about issues that we would never have discussed without Picoult's urging us to by writing about such controversial topics.  As a high school teacher, I was particularly touched by Nineteen Minutes, a novel through which Picoult made me see in a different light the issues surrounding a school shooting.    But I'm afraid Picoult has let me down this time. In her previous novels, Picoult's characters hold varied political, social, and theological ideas and ideals, but I have never been sure which way Picoult herself leans.  That is definitely not the case in Sing You Home.  This time, Picoult's political agenda shows way too much!!  Her characters usually possess great depth and dimension, but this time, they are all \\"flat,\\" and one-dimensional, defined by their position on gay rights.  The Christians, in particular, are so flatly drawn that they are just \\"stock\\" characters.  The one exception to the flatness occurs in Liddy, but in this case, Liddy engages in a behavior at one point in the novel that defies the very one-sidedness of her.  It's totally unbelievable that Liddy would do this.  The novel reads as if Picoult did the research (which she usually does incredibly well) on the political and social leanings of the gay couple and the characters in their camp, but absolutely no research on what true Christians are really like and instead relied on negative stereotypes.  I'm amazed sometimes that when people are faced with others who do not hold their same viewpoints and beliefs and values that they label the others as closed-minded, and, yet, the people who share their viewpoints are open-minded.  They scream about intolerance and are some of the most intolerant people in the world.  Zoe falls in that category.  She is probably the most intolerant character in the whole novel.  Her vehemence toward Liddy and ALL Christians is intolerance personoified.    One final note about the book that has nothing to do with Picoult's political agenda is this:  Lucy.  First of all, as intelligent readers, we figured out who Lucy is, who her parents are, early in the novel, and yet Picoult treated the subject as if we were supposed to be surprised when she revealed it.  Secondly, the Lucy storyline was left as a major loose end.  Picoult usually ties things up better than that.    Although this novel was a huge disappointment, I shall look foward to her next one.  I enjoy an author who makes me think and gives me fodder for discussion with friends.  I just hope she won't disappoint me again.

0393338827|5.0|I don't normally review business literature but Michael Lewis' account of the 2008 Wall Street Collapse was so well rendered in his book, \\"The Big Short,\\" that I felt compelled to review it for its succinct description of a complex phenomenon and because Lewis achieves a literary voice and psychological depth more akin to literary fiction than financial analysis.  He is a smooth, clear, imaginative writer who conveys the facts as well as the psychological underpinnings of the banking system debacle.  In doing so, he portrays several key figures whose personalities and grit drove the bust and profited from it, as well as others whose insights could provide fodder to statesmen initiating regulatory reform.  If capitalism is to survive without continued erosion of the middle class, restrictions must be devised to protect investors, home purchasers, insurance companies and the stock market in general.  Everything must be done to assure that there are no incentives to bet against what these institutions stand for.  If shorting is widespread, the country suffers.  With so much at stake in the world of economics and so many members of the workplace identifying with global institutions rather than America per se, policies must be put in place to assure sound business practices that benefit America rather than encourage fraud, manipulation and greed.<br /><br />In identifying the fraud of sub-prime mortgages as a primary cause for the collapse, Lewis employs the metaphor of one lonely, somewhat anti-social neurologist interning at Stanford University Hospital.  Dr. Michael Burry symbolizes the fallacies of the stock market in general as well as the housing bubble's destructive yet predictable course.  In characterizing Burry, Lewis suggests the hubris necessary either to manipulate the market or to expect to profit from it.  After all, Burry was a genius endowed with intellectual capabilities most of us do not have - an understanding that was clearly lacking in the major Wall Street firms.  Burry realized early on that a housing crisis loomed due to the widespread practice of sub-prime loans and their shadowy, insubstantial backing by bonds that were equally complicated and flimsy.  Assured that the housing market could not sustain itself, he sought a way to bet against the sub-prime mortgages.  An intern with long hours, he was a busy man, but his obsession with the world of investing drove him to reading the legalese of sub-prime loan documents and thereby devising a plan to profit from the impending housing crisis.<br /><br />By 2005 no one would sell loans on sub-prime mortgages because the interest rates would eventually climb as the number of bank defaults on sub-prime mortgages increased.  So Burry convinced Morgan Stanley, Deutche Bank and Goldman-Sachs to sell him credit default swaps  that would serve as insurance against losses resulting from the dubious practice of sub-prime mortgage lending.  In the process, he lost several of his clients who feared his unorthodox practices.  While his clients were unwilling to invest their money as a tool for Burry's betting short, it was Burry's grasp of the inevitability of the housing crisis that drove him to switch directions in his investing protocol.  As it turned out, he made millions for his clients and himself by betting against Wall Street.  The metaphor here, while it cannot be separated from the idea of American ego, clearly suggests that the typical speculative investor should heed the warning that not all of us are as intelligent and as driven as  a Michael Burry.  As Lewis so deftly points out, none of our financial institutions possessed his insight.<br /><br />Lewis brilliantly juxtaposes Burry's monomaniacal search for the perfect investment opportunity with his gradual understanding of his own mysterious, complex personality.  Burry had always assumed that his intellectual gifts were the result of his having lost an eye to a rare kind of cancer when he was a child.  Dueto his physical handicap, he did not look people in the eye, was regarded as different and found himself more interested in his own intellectual pursuits than cultivating associates.  A loner, his obsessive interest with the financial world drove him to understand what Lewis admits, that he, a stockbroker, Princeton graduate, hedge fund operator himself, did not.  In one of the most interesting passages in the book, Lewis describes how the intelligent, critical thinking graduates of Ivy League colleges secure employment in large investment firms, having never mastered the rudiments of accounting and thereby proceed to carry out their jobs without an understanding of the complex world of high finance they inhabit.  Again and again, Lewis exposes the lack of knowledge those who run Wall Street epitomize, from the lenders, to the hedge fund managers, the analysts/appraisers at Moody's and Standard and Poors, to the heads of large investment firms such as Goldman-Sachs, Morgan Stanley, and Bear-Stearns.  Burry understood the system's fallacies while they did not so he obsessively proceeded to grasp the paradoxes of the marketplace until he was ready to quit medicine and start up his own investment company, Scion.<br /><br />Interestingly enough Michael Burry went to medical school because it was easy for him, not because he was dedicated to medicine.  In fact, hospital life both bored him and disgusted him.  Instead high finance had always been his interest, even though his own father discouraged involvement in the stock market.  When his father died, he founded Scion and proceeded to do what he wanted with his life.  Early on he realized that \\"no one could teach you how to invest.\\"  It was a talent he felt he had and so he pursued it.  Thus, the metaphor of Burry as well as Lewis' tendency to portray characters in depth transforms what could be a dull read into a psychologically compelling experience.<br /><br />Joel Greenblatt among others encouraged Burry and invested in his company.  Others were to follow Burry's lead in purchasing credit default swaps to ameliorate the losses of sub-prime mortgages.  Burry's M.O. was to pick the worst of the sub-prime mortgages and bet against them with the purchase of credit default swaps.  Goldman-Sachs and other firms failed to understand why he continued to purchase swaps.  And although housing delinquencies hit an all time high and thus Burry's protocol made sense monetarily, his own investors were fearful of his practice of betting against the housing market.  Yet Wall Street was slowly waking up; by 2007 sub-prime mortgage defaults were at an all time high, with Bear-Stearns going under and Goldman-Sachs experiencing major losses.  At this time Burry's own investors were making a profit of almost 500% but they still did not trust him.  This fact illustrated the basic lack of confidence some people had in Burry.  This lack of trust Burry attributed to his inability to look people in the eye.  While Burry clearly understood the casino-like nature of investing in sub-prime mortgage bonds and derivatives, others, including the magnates on Wall Street, had no idea of the insubstantiality of their practices and proceeded to game the system, as did Burry, yet Burry and a few others understood the system while most major players did not.<br /><br />At the height of the financial crisis, Burry realized that his son had Asperger's Syndrome.  The more he thought about it, his son's diagnosis explained his own personality as well.  When he acknowledged that, he began to understand the strange compulsions that had driven him to make sense of the financial world.  In realizing his obsession, he is a metaphor for the individual whose visionary characteristics can be instrumental in drastic paradigm shifts.  Thus is Michael Burry a hero to Lewis and others who study the market.  Through understanding his actions and by interviewing him, Lewis and others acknowledged the genius of this lone wolf whosebrain should be picked to seal off the holes in predatory lending and investing practices that if unchecked, could drive our nation to ruin.<br /><br />By clarifying Wall Street practices and explaining such speculative ventures as \\"tranches,\\" Lewis makes clear, not only the risks of investing for the ordinary man whose skills are not advanced and whose knowledge of the intricacies of finance is not honed, but also the greed and predatory systems that exist to maintain Wall Street dominance.  In this regard, Wall Street institutions obscured what needed to be clarified in a self-serving attempt to preserve themselves at the cost of individual investors as well as the American people.  As Burry observed, Wall Street standards had not just deteriorated but were non-existent.  By amortizing sub-prime mortgages at adjustable rates, Wall Street had created a disastrous real estate bubble which the American tax payer would have to absorb if the financial system was to survive.  Burry believed lending institutions should show restraint.  Yet they clearly were not doing so, instead betting against their own loans to assure profits.  In his view it was not only an immoral system but ultimately an unsustainable one.  As Burry predicted, it resulted in the tanking of the American economy even as it solidified Goldman-Sachs and other questionable entities.<br /><br />Lewis points out that in the middle of the crisis, most Wall Street firms were tottering on the brink of collapse.  \\"The world's most powerful and highly paid financiers had been entirely discredited; without government intervention every single one of them would have lost his job, and yet those same financial advisers that had steered America to collapse were still using the government to enrich themselves,\\" Lewis points out.  He quotes Burry, \\"What I can't understand is why anyone would listen to them.\\"<br /><br />Steve Eisman, another interesting and prime player in the debacle, exposed the deceptions of dozens of companies, calling the Household Finance Corporation's practices \\"blatant fraud.\\"  Said Eisman, \\"Wall Street didn't give a s--- what it sold.\\"  Moreover, Eisman claimed \\"Greenspan will go down as the worst head of the Federal Reserve in history.\\"  By using quotes and taking the time to fully portray the characters in this dismal scenario, Lewis makes the book of complex financial practices clear and interesting because we see real people involved in the process.  The Eisman Lewis depicts was a rude man, so people were reluctant to listen to him.  But as Lewis makes clear it was Eisman's brilliance that permitted him to realize that \\"gambling and investing is artificial and thin.\\"  Driven by greed, claims Lewis, most players fail to understand the complexity of the practices they promote.  It is not tenable for the less than brilliant to venture into such an arcane world as speculative investments  --  sub-prime mortgage bonds, CDO's or credit default swaps.  It takes a genius to know what you're doing in such a complicated situation.  Most fall prey to Group Think, which is the pattern of Investment Banking in general.  One has to watch out for the sharks, but also for the stupid or uninformed.<br /><br />Thus is the significance of the message Lewis delivers to those who will listen.  Let us hope Lewis is not the Cassandra of America, warning of economic destruction while no one listens.  This is a book to take to heart, to insist your children and friends read and read again.  It is intelligent, heartfelt, wise, and literary.  Reading it will sober anyone as to the risks we so unwittingly take on as part of our cultural heritage.  The obvious lesson of this book is to avoid wading into deep waters if your swimming expertise is marginal.  There are some things most of us are not equipped to understand but our insufferable egos drive us on and on to failure and loss.  Maybe unbridled Laissez- fairre simply doesn't work after all, at least not for the average guy.  Wha'dya say, Michael?<br /><br />Marjorie Meyerle<br />Colorado Writer<br />Author of [[ASIN:1440149577 Bread of Shame]]holes in predatory lending and investing practices that if unchecked, could drive our nation to ruin. B004NSULHM|3.0|I keep hearing worshippers of Radiohead implore the band's critics to look past the music to appreciate its deeper meaning, to grasp the nuances of Radiohead's profound message. Here's the deeper meaning of King of Limbs, as far as I can tell. The new music industry is dead, having been killed by Napster as the movie, The Social Network, points out. Radiohead now looks forward to a world in which music is created without a viable business model. (Most of the money in music comes from CD sales, not from concerts.) In that world, music ought to be disposable to punish the thieving fans who don't value art enough to pay for it. Thus, with the faster songs on this new CD, Radiohead is giving people what they secretly want or at least what they deserve: worthless, vacuous, disposable, easily-forgotten anti-music. King of Limbs is largely a neo-Muzak dirge for new music.      Why try hard to create fresh and inspiring melodies when the consumers' actions indicate that the consumers don't care, even though they may hypocritically say otherwise? We're so decadent and infantilized that we expect new music for free, to flow from the internet to our MP3 players like water from the ocean. Why should artists suffer in their creative processes to cater to spoiled consumers who soon won't even know what we're missing, because our attention span is vanishingly small and we've got a hole where our memory should be?     Of course, art appreciation is mostly subjective. As the philosopher Daniel Dennett likes to say, humans are excellent pattern detectors. You can find what you like in any pattern at all, so fans of Radiohead will have no trouble seeing in this new release what they want to find. It's like John Edwards' psychic shenanigans, which are really just a game of 20 questions: the questioned people do most of the associative work in their own heads, and the questioner just ties things up with a nice bow. Different sounds will resonate with different people, depending on the unique, labyrinthine neural connections we bring with us.      But music's ability to evoke emotions is more objective, since that requires tapping into an older, more universal and hardwired part of our brain. That's why horror movies use the same kind of skin-crawling sounds to evoke fear, while romantic movies use soaring violins to evoke cathartic feelings of love. The main feeling evoked by the fast songs on King of Limbs is boredom, which is what we deserve for adoring the new communications technologies that are fatal to art.      Is this intentional on Radiohead's part? Does the band loathe its fans? Of course not. But the meaning of art goes beyond either the artist's intentions or the consumer's idiosyncratic interpretations. The ultimate meaning has to do with where the art fits into a society that has reached a particular stage. Unfortunately, American-led society is in decline, and meaningful art in that society speaks to that decline.     There's another factor too. Artists do their best work when they're young and desperate, not when they're older and successful. Thus, artists are microcosms of societies in that they each pass through stages of vitality and of corruption.    ****    After listening to KOL a number of times more after my initial review, the last few songs on the CD have grown on me, so I've changed my rating from 2 to 3 stars. I'm still unhappy with the faster songs on the CD: they're not as memorable as the fast ones from other Radiohead CDs, they don't rock, and they don't go anywhere. Here's a song-by-song reflection.     Bloom - This is one of the more interesting songs on the CD, in my view. I like the chirping that sounds like a flock of birds in the middle of the third minute, and the violins and horn background. So as an atmospheric song with no melody, it's pretty good; it holds my attention and I expect it will do so after repeated listening.    Morning Mr Magpie - By contrast, this song is boring: no interesting instruments,and just the repetitive chanting of mantras (as opposed to the singing of a melody) with muted drums.     Little by little - Like most songs on KOL, this song's variations happen within narrow parameters. The song has no beginning, middle, or end, and instead of a melody, the song is made up of chanting with some percussive accompaniment. The song isn't horrible, but I can't imagine being interested in listening to it over and over again. That's the same as saying that the song already begins to bore me.    Feral - A throwaway filler song on a really short CD, the objectively worst on KOL. Experimental, nonsensical, repetitive, atmospheric noises that vary within narrow parameters and go nowhere. I won't want to listen to this song over and over again, unlike most Radiohead songs.    Lotus Flower - Compared to the faster songs on Radiohead's other, recent CDs, this song doesn't rock and it's not as memorable. The song's not boring, but it's not powerful either.    Codex - A fine song, although perhaps a lesser version of Piano Song. Unlike the faster songs on KOL, this one isn't a disposable digital mess for thieving fans.      Give up the mess - A great transition near the end, and I like the way Yorke distorts some of his singing to sound like the big horns they blow in Tibetan monasteries.     Seperator - This song has hooked me after repeated listening.     Again, after repeated listening, I've come to like some of the songs on KOL, but I still don't think this CD is as good as any of Radiohead's other CDs, because of its inconsistency and brevity (only 8 songs).

B0047Y17P2|5.0|I have noticed two consistent components in Michael Connelly's works. First, he thoroughly plans his story before he writes. Other writers, it seems to me, often write as they go. That is, it appears that their story shifts significantly at some point well after the beginning, and the author, perhaps, is too lazy or too rushed to go back and carefully realign. How often have you encountered characters or plot developments in fiction land that are introduced and then abandoned?  I see none of this in Connelly's novels, dating back to the first Harry Bosch books.     Another common trait is that the author drops a clue early on in the novel that readers may at first see as significant, but may neglect as the story progresses. I know I have missed such clues in Connelly's works.  There's a stunning development late in this novel that fits this pattern.     A key figure in Fifth Witness is Lisa Trammel. She is in danger of losing her home, caught up in the foreclosure mess that has plagued the country in recent years. She and her husband purchased a home that was likely well beyond their means in 2005. The loan included a massive balloon payment due in five years. When Trammel's husband disappeared, her sole income as a teacher is not enough to keep her, her young son, and her expensive home afloat. She can't cover the balloon payment. She fights the foreclosure process because she is unable to refinance due to her home's plummeting value. Trammel becomes an outspoken advocate for those who are fighting foreclosure. She attracts significant media attention.    Enter Mickey Haller, the \\"Lincoln Lawyer\\" who has become the leading character in recent Connelly books.(Harry Bosch has a micro cameo appearance here.) Haller has shifted from criminal law to representing people who are fighting foreclosure. There are legions of them across the country. These foreclosures often involve deceptive/fraudulent practices that a skillful lawyer like Haller can uncover and use to his clients' advantage. One of Haller's clients is Lisa Trammel. Haller was her attorney when she was arrested and charged with the murder of Mitchell Bondurant, senior vice president of the mortgage company that holds Trammel's note. Trammel may hate Bondurant, but did she murder him? That is the question.    I don't want to reveal anything about Haller's investigation of the murder or about the trial. It's all fascinating. I did not find an extraneous word in the book. And there's that smashing twist at the end alluded to above. I don't think I'll ever forget the resolution Connelly invents for a pivotal trial issue.    Another strong point is the spin that Connelly puts on the classic lawyer rationale for defending those who are actually guilty of the crime. The chant is: Actual guilt or innocence is not important. The accused must be found guilty in a court of law.  I have always had a hard time accepting this rationale when lawyers grow wealthy representing  immoral, vicious people. Connelly's analysis of all of this is the best that I have encountered in fiction land.    If you have read this far into this review you may wonder what the review's title means. I assure you that it will become clear as you finish the novel.

B001152TL6|5.0|I saw my first Lynch film last fall (Mulholland Dr) and quickly became fascinated with this perplexing, warped and brilliant director. His earlier work, such as Blue Velvet and Wild at Heart, presents an interesting mix of campy melodrama and brutally violent realism, which is interesting but somewhat jarring as the two formulas don't quite seem to compliment each other.<br /><br />Lost Highway is a different kind of Lynch film, completely consistent in tone and (I would argue) coherent in structure and philosophy. I haven't seen Fire Walk With Me, but it's clear that Lynch took a major step forward between Wild at Heart and this one. Lost Highway moves slowly but steadily, weaving a sense of foreboding that doesn't let up even when the worst has apparently happened. The tension is unremitting, and even the humor (in a neat trick that is distinctly Lynchian) does nothing to alleviate the sense of menace.<br /><br />There are a lot of different interpretations of the story, but the one I find most convincing is that we are watching the main character's mind construct a fantasy to protect him from the knowledge that he killed his wife, while an uglier but more honest part of his mind tries to shove that very same fact into his face. There is not necessarily a right answer, but I think this one explains a lot of things very elegantly. In any case, there are plenty of clues in the film to help you form a coherent idea of what it's about, and as long as you enjoy this sort of challenge I think you'll enjoy Lost Highway as well.

0399250573|5.0|I have read several of the reviews and noticed \\"disappointed\\" popping up quite often.  If you are looking for a Disney, everyone is happy ending, then yes-you will be disappointed. If you are looking for an authentic ending to a teenager's career as a spy, involving saving the world nine times in one year, then read it.    The story telling alone was amazing. I had thought I was past the time in my life of reading a thick novel in two days. Apparently, I had not picked one up as well written and woven with suspense as \\"Scorpia Rising.\\" Each chapter gave me a reason to continue until I was in the climax, screaming from suspense and knowing that, despite my wishes to savor the last Alex Rider book, I had to finish it. Now.    ****SPOILERS IN REVIEW***    As with all the Alex Rider books, the story follows the basic plot-first chapter murder, villain out to get the world or England, MI6 has a lead that they need Alex to follow, Alex is caught up in the whirr of things and meets the villain face to face, etc. As essential to Alex as a murder is to a murder mystery.  But this time Anthony Horowitz threw in a twist. We begin by following the villain for several chapters as he lays out a trap for MI6, or more specifically, for Alex Rider. The beginning, although I was itching to get to Alex, was refreshing. It set the mood for the rest of the story. I wanted to scream at Alex, and MI6, for falling into their plan all during the rest of the story.    Anthony Horowitz had said several things about this book-he would kill off a character that had been in all of the books and that Alex would end up worse than dead.  *****EXTRA SPOILER*****  Throughout the book, I was continually wondering who would die. I knew it would not be Alex, I didn't care if it was Blunt, so that left three people who could die that I would care about-Mrs. Jones, Smithers, and Jack. Don't let it be Jack....please? It was. I hoped that her death was faked, but knowing the villain, it could not be, and itwas not.  I cannot imagine Mr. Horowitz rubbing his hands with glee as he killed a favorite character-I believe it was as hard for him to write as it was for me to read. The death was well written, and necessary almost, considering where the story had gone. It would be too happy if all his loved ones-Jack, Sabina and family, and friends-had made it unscathed through his adventures. And, besides preventing Alex from a horrible death or at the least, becoming a cripple for the rest of his life, it was the moment when he changed from the boy spy to a \\"grown up\\" spy.  He would never spy again. No matter what.  The end was a relief. I was so happy for Alex to be able to leave England behind, MI6 behind, and finally have the most normal life possible with Sabina. He needed that. The readers needed hope that Alex will not commit suicide or sit in a daze for the rest of his life.  Due to the death, his age, his size, and the fact that he slipped up a few times, Alex will not spy anymore.  *****END SPOILERS****    I think that this book was a perfect ending for the series. Darker, yes, but that is part of the point. Spying is not for kids, it never should have been. Considering what I had imagined would happen to Alex, I was slightly relieved. And Anthony Horowitz allowed us some hope for Alex, but not for any more Alex Rider Spy books. This book surpassed the last one and is up with my top favorites-Scorpia, Snakehead, and Skeleton Key.    Oh, and by the way, Smither's gadget is amazing. The best. You will know what I am talking about when you read it.

B00005QGAX|5.0|Bad. The album that came after the great Thriller. How do you top Thriller? Bad, sadly, did not top Thriller in terms of sales, but musically, it is better than Thriller. I mean it. Let's take a look.    1. Bad. An exciting start to the album. Originally intended to be a duet between MJ and Prince, Prince decided not to go along with the project because he didn't want to sing the line \\"Your butt is mine\\" to MJ. MJ himself described the lyrics as follows,    \\"'Bad' is a song about the street. It's about this kid from a bad neighborhood who gets to go away to a private school. He comes back to the old neighborhood when he's on a break from school and the kids from the neighborhood start giving him trouble. He sings, 'I'm bad, you're bad, who's bad, who's the best?' He's saying when you're strong and good, then you're bad.\\"    Right from the start, this song grabs you and prepares you for the rest of the album. It's edgy, it's funky, it's... MJ.    2. The Way You Make Me Feel. Another explosive piece. This song really captures the youthful energy of MJ at the time, and this piece makes you feel happy and upbeat every time you listen to it. An absolute classic. 10/10    3. Speed Demon. What is this? A song about driving fast? MJ said that he wrote this piece when Quincy Jones told him to write a song about the time when he was pulled over while going to the recording studio. He sings about this topic only once, here on Speed Demon. Now, this song is really great to listen to when you're driving on the highway. Quite an unusual song. He never, and I repeat, never sings about driving ever again, but here he does. This song ... I really don't know how to describe this piece. It's unusual... Nevertheless, the beat is great, the lyrics are great, and well, the whole song is great. 8/10    4. Liberian Girl. Written in 1983 and considered for the Victory Album, it was reworked and rewritten and appeared on Bad. Quite an exotic piece, the song starts with a South African singerby the name of Letta Mbulu saying a Swahili phrase \\"Naku Penda pia, Naku Taka pia, Mpenziwe!\\" (I love you too, I want you too, my love!). One word describes this piece. SEXY. I usually compare this piece to Butterflies, because they are quiet, yet both songs ooze passion. Passion just flows right out and hits you. Sexy, smooth, it's a relaxing piece. I LOVE it. 10/10    5. Just Good Friends. A duet with Stevie Wonder, it is the first song on this album NOT written or composed by Michael. I have heard some call this duet \\"heavenly\\", and some call this a \\"filler\\". Even though I acknowledge Stevie Wonder's voice is \\"heavenly\\", I feel that this song is the only \\"filler\\" on this album. 6/10    6. Another Part Of Me. An explosive, happy piece which gives you energy. I absolutely love this piece, and I always return to this piece when I am extremely melancholy. 9/10    7. Man In The Mirror. The first MJ song to deal with global issues, this was written by Siedah Garrett and Glen Ballard. An amazing piece, this song is really immortal because of it's finely crafted production, Michael's vocals, and the topic of the piece. One of Michael's best pieces ever. 10/10    8. I Just Can't Stop Loving You. Heavenly piece. Originally, the song began with Michael talking, but that was later cut. What a bad move. The spoken intro makes the song even more heavenly. Anyways, a duet between Siedah Garrett and MJ, Siedah's vocals and Michael's vocals mingle with one another to give the piece a heavenly effect, and makes you feel so relaxed and happy. The very beginning of the piece gives me the imagery of sitting on an open field with your loved one and watching the sun rise over a distant hill. And then, the romance begins...   One of my favorites. 10/10    9. Dirty Diana. An absolute contrast to the soft piece right before. A rough, edgy piece, the topic is similar to the topics covered by Billie Jean, and Who Is It. But that's where the similarity ends. Whereas Billie Jean and Who Is It are both soft, quiet, with emotion simmering under the surface, this piece just explodes and the emotion pierces the surface. 10/10    10. Smooth Criminal. A MJ classic. I'm sure everyone, and I mean EVERYONE knows the Smooth Criminal video with the anti-gravity lean! The lyrics ask frantically, \\"Annie are you OK?\\". Well, I want to know, Annie, ARE you OK? Anyways, the song is powerful, and it just explodes everywhere. Words cannot adequately describe the piece. Just buy the album and listen for yourself. The greatest way to end an album. 10/10    11. Leave Me Alone. Even though this piece didn't come with the original Bad album, I feel this is worth reviewing because of it's topic: the media. I feel that Leave Me Alone and the later Tabloid Junkie (in HIStory) do the best job of attacking the media and how they sensationalize stories. 10/10     This gives a total score of 103/110, which is 94%, which means this is a GREAT album.    * * *    While Thriller was Michael's commercial achievement, Bad is really more of a musical achievement. The songs range from sexy, to tough, to energetic, to frantic, to rough. Even though some people consider Thriller to be Michael's best album, I wholeheartedly disagree. Bad is a musical album. 0451228375|4.0|First of all, the type is too small. I'd been scoffing at those strangely-shaped too-tall paperbacks that allow bigger type for boomer's aging eyes. This is the first time I wish a publisher had used it. At age 65, this is the very first time I've encountered a paperback that I found difficult to read due to type size. I certainly wish that they'd foregone the sample teaser chapter at the end and had used the extra space to make the type just a big more legible. If I were going to read it again, I'd get the hardbound from the library instead of trying to read the paperback.    Now the review.    This is a thoroughly enjoyable page-turner, and it certainly brought the Middle Ages alive for me. By all means, read it, especially if you know you like Ken Follett. I loved it. But, like the medieval image of the spinning Wheel of Fortune, the plot spins all too mechanically, rather like a soap opera in print. The book is populated with a handful of human characters that you really do care about, and a comic-book background of sociopathic villains who exist, seemingly for no other purpose than to make the main characters' lives miserable.    The book feels almost like a linked series of short stories or novellas. Every episode contains some little detail that seems important, but you are not quite sure why. The main characters surmount a terrible obstacle, and just as you think everything is going to be OK, you realize that the strangely salient detail was a fuse that was lit in order for a bomb to explode into the next crisis.  Lather, rinse, repeat. Worst of all, the evil villains in many cases act against their own self-interest, as if their success is less important to them than spoiling things for the Good Guys.    Follett takes particular care to recapitulate past bits of the plot as needed and re-identify characters as they reappear. This is a much-appreciated courtesy to the relaxed reader who is simply reading for pleasure. I never had trouble remembering characters or disentangling intertwined plot threads. Although it's a long book, you don't have to read it all in one piece. You can put it down for a day or two, pick it up, and not get lost--Follett steers you right back.    The central characters have too much of a 21st-century sensibility to feel right. I just don't know. On the whole I think it is more likely that human nature is what it is, and it is closer to the truth to make the interior life of a 1300's prioress the same as that of a modern woman than than to believe it was utterly alien. Still, it is a little strange to see people of that era taking such modern attitudes toward gender roles, sexual orientation, evidence-based medicine, free-market economics... No, Caris (the nurse/heroine) doesn't actually start wondering if fleas cause plague, or discovering that that green mold is great for healing wounds--it's not _that_ bad. But, I dunno, I just think he goes a little too far.
